5 min
Refutation of Interpreting Qadar as Creation of Pleasures and Pains
Refutation of a narrow interpretation of qadar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that belief in qadar must refer to moral actions, not merely natural pleasures and pains.

Core Idea
Qadar is about moral actions, not just natural causes.

Some interpret qadar as God creating pleasures and pains—natural events. The author refutes this by pointing out that the shahada already affirms God's creation of benefits and harms, so qadar in the hadith must mean something additional: that moral acts (obedience and sin) are from God. This lesson unpacks that concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the compact argument structure.
The opponent's view is stated first, then refuted.
2
Identify the opponent's view
The opponent claims that 'belief in qadar' means 'God creates pleasures and pains.' This is what the author rejects.
3
Understand the author's evidence
The author uses the shahada (لا إله إلا الله) as proof. Why does that negate the opponent's interpretation? Because the shahada already declares God as the sole creator of benefits and harms.
This shows the opponent's view is redundant.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the shahada already covers benefits/harms, qadar must mean something else: that acts of obedience and evil are from God. This expands qadar to moral actions.
5
Reflect on the significance
Why does this matter for divine transcendence? It affirms God's sovereignty over all actions, including moral choices, without negating human responsibility.
This is key in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : « الإيمان بالقدر ، محمول على أن خالق اللذات والآلام ، هو الله ، قلنا : هذا ضعيف . لأن قولنا : لا إله إلا الله ، تصريح بنفي الإلهين وذلك يدل على أن خالق المنافع والمضار، هو الله . فلما أوجب بعده الإيمان بالقدر ، وجب أن يكون المراد من القدر شيئاً آخر ، سوى ذلك . وما ذاك إلا الإيمان بأن الطاعات والشرور كلها من الله .
As for his saying: 'Belief in qadar is interpreted as that the creator of pleasures and pains is God,' we say: this is weak. Because our saying 'There is no god but God' is a declaration negating the two gods, and that indicates that the creator of benefits and harms is God. So when He made belief in qadar obligatory after that, it is necessary that the intended meaning of qadar be something other than that. And that is nothing but the belief that acts of obedience and evils are all from God.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
Here the author refutes the interpretation that qadar means that God is the creator of pleasures and pains. The author argues that the shahada (لا إله إلا الله) already affirms that God is the creator of benefits and harms, so belief in qadar, which comes after the shahada in the hadith, must refer to something additional. That additional meaning is that acts of obedience and disobedience (evils) are all from God (i.e., created by Him). Thus qadar is not merely about natural pleasures and pains but about moral actions.

Key Terms
لا إله إلا الله
إلهين
منافع ومضار
طاعات وشرور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qadar only means God creates natural pleasures and pains, not moral actions.
Correction: The author argues that qadar specifically includes that acts of obedience and evil are from God, going beyond natural events.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the interpretation that qadar means God creates pleasures and pains?
A) Because pleasures and pains are not created by God.
B) Because the shahada already states that, so qadar must mean something else.
C) Because the hadith forbids it.
D) Because pleasures and pains are from the servant.

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that qadar must include the divine origin of moral actions, not just natural pleasures and pains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you truly believe that both your obedience and your disobedience are from God? How does that belief affect the way you approach repentance and your trust in His mercy?
?
The author argues that the shahada already affirms God as the creator of your successes and failures. Have you ever lived with that awareness moment by moment? What would change in your daily attitude if you consistently remembered that every benefit and harm comes from Him alone?
?
When you sin, do you quickly attribute it to your own free will, or do you humbly recognize that it too is under God's decree? Which perspective leads you to genuine humility and which to self-excuse? Be honest with yourself.

Refuting the Claim that Qadar is from the Servant and Clarifying Ali's Position
Refutation of two objections regarding qadar from the servant and Ali's stance on jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes two objections regarding qadar from the servant and Ali's stance on jabr.

Core Idea
Qadar belongs entirely to Allah, and Ali's position is not against that.

This passage addresses two final objections from opponents. First, they claim that the hadith means qadar originates from the servant; the author counters by pointing to a variant narration that explicitly attributes qadar to Allah. Second, they invoke Ali ibn Abi Talib's supposed rejection of jabr to reinterpret the hadith; the author rejects this claim and promises to prove Ali's true position later. This lesson clarifies these points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand the objections and responses.
Notice the structure: 'As for his saying... we say...'.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage contains two objections from opponents. Find each and note how the author responds.
The first objection is about qadar from the servant; the second about Ali's stance.
3
Analyze the author's method
The author uses a hadith variant to refute the first objection and defers the discussion of Ali's stance. Consider why this approach is effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : ( المراد من ذلك : إثبات أن القدر من العبد ، قلنا : هذا مدفوع بما أنه جاء في بعض الروايات : « وأن تؤمن بأن القدر خيره وشره من الله . وأما قوله : ( مذهب علي بن أبي طالب، نفي الجبر ، والراوي إذا خالف روايته دل على ضعف في الرواية ، قلنا : لا نسلم أن مذهب علي » ما ذكرتم . وسيأتي تقريره ، إن شاء الله تعالى .
As for his saying: 'The intended meaning of that is affirming that qadar is from the servant,' we say: this is refuted because it has come in some narrations: 'And that you believe that qadar, its good and its evil, is from God.' As for his saying: 'The madhhab of Ali ibn Abi Talib is the negation of jabr, and if the narrator contradicts his narration, it indicates weakness in the narration,' we say: we do not concede that the madhhab of Ali is what you have mentioned. And its confirmation will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage addresses two final objections. First, the objector claims that the hadith means qadar is from the servant. The author refutes this by citing another version of the hadith that explicitly states qadar is from God. Second, the objector argues that 'Ali ibn Abi Talib's stance was against jabr (compulsion), and since he narrated the hadith, the hadith should be interpreted accordingly. The author rejects this claim, stating that 'Ali's madhhab is not as alleged, and promises to provide evidence later.

Key Terms
رواية
قدر من العبد
قدر من الله
مذهب علي
جبر
راوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that qadar is from the servant, meaning humans create their own actions independently.
Correction: The author refutes this by citing a hadith that explicitly attributes qadar, both good and evil, to Allah alone.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that qadar is from the servant?
A) By logical argument.
B) By citing another hadith that says qadar is from God.
C) By quoting Ali's opinion.
D) By appealing to consensus.

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that hadith variants can serve as decisive evidence in theological debates, and that deferring a complex issue to a later discussion is a legitimate argumentative strategy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a critique of your faith without verifying the primary sources? This lesson shows that hadith variants can change the meaning. Are you willing to check the original texts before accepting a claim that challenges your belief?
?
When someone attributes a view to a righteous figure like Ali, do you accept it without investigation? This lesson reveals that even great scholars' positions can be misrepresented. How will you protect yourself from such misrepresentations in the future?
?
The author defers a full explanation of Ali's madhhab, demonstrating humility and trust in sustained study. When you encounter a difficult theological issue, do you rush to a conclusion or patiently seek deeper knowledge? Commit to taking one specific step this week to deepen your understanding of a contested issue.

Qadr is Not Writing in the Preserved Tablet
The author argues that qadr is not writing in the Preserved Tablet or exposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the qadr mentioned in the hadith is not the writing in the Preserved Tablet or any form of exposition.

Core Idea
Qadr in the hadith is not an external record but a belief about God's decree.

The author begins by establishing that belief in qadr is a condition of sound faith. He then argues logically that the qadr referred to cannot be the writing in the Preserved Tablet or any exposition to humans or angels. This lesson clarifies the nature of qadr as a matter of faith, not a physical entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure.
The author uses a logical syllogism (second figure).
2
Identify the two refutations
The author first refutes the idea that qadr is writing in the Preserved Tablet, then that it is exposition.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on why this distinction matters for correct belief.
Belief in qadr is about trust in God's decree, not about a book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن الإيمان بالقدر من شرائط صحة الإيمان. وهذا ينتج من الشكل الثاني : أنه ليس المراد من القدر المذكور في هذا الخبر هو الكتابة في اللوح المحفوظ : وأما حمل القدر على العلم واللسان ، فباطل أيضاً . لأن ذلك البيان ، إما أن يكون بياناً للبشر ، وهو مفقود . أو للملائكة ، وهو أيضاً باطل . لأن العلم بأن الله بين أحوال أفعال العباد للملائكة ليس من واجبات الإيمان . وإيمان بالقدر من واجبات الإيمان بحكم دلالة هذا الخبر . وذلك يدل على أنه ليس المراد من هذا القدر هو البيان .
وأما حمل القدر على العلم ، فضعيف . لأنه إن حمل على أي علم كان ، فهو باطل . بدليل : أن من علم ذات الله وصفاته . لا يقال : إنه قدر ذات الله وصفاته ، وإن حمل على العلم المقتضي إيقاع الفعل على وجه خاص ، فهذا يقتضي أن لا يحصل القدر إلا مع التخليق والتكوين . وإن حمل على علم الله بكيفية أفعال العباد ، فالجبر أيضاً لازم. لما ثبت أن خلاف معلوم الله : محال
الوقوع .
وأما قوله : « الإيمان بالقدر ، محمول على أن خالق اللذات والآلام ، هو الله ، قلنا : هذا ضعيف . لأن قولنا : لا إله إلا الله ، تصريح بنفي الإلمين وذلك يدل على أن خالق المنافع والمضار، هو الله . فلما أوجب بعده الإيمان بالقدر ، وجب أن يكون المراد من القدر شيئاً آخر ، سوى ذلك . وما ذاك إلا الإيمان بأن الطاعات والشرور كلها من الله .
وأما قوله : ( المراد من ذلك : إثبات أن القدر من العبد ، قلنا : هذا مدفوع بما أنه جاء في بعض الروايات : « وأن تؤمن بأن القدر خيره وشره من
الله .
وأما قوله : ( مذهب علي بن أبي طالب، نفي الجبر ، والراوي إذا خالف روايته دل على ضعف في الرواية ، قلنا : لا نسلم أن مذهب و علي » ما ذكرتم . وسيأتي تقريره ، إن شاء الله تعالى .
That belief in qadr is among the conditions of sound faith. And this results from the second figure: that what is meant by 'qadr' mentioned in this report is not the writing in the Preserved Tablet. And as for interpreting qadr as knowledge and the tongue, it is also false. Because that exposition is either an exposition to humans, which is absent, or to angels, which is also false. Because the knowledge that God explained the states of the actions of servants to the angels is not among the obligations of faith. And belief in qadr is among the obligations of faith by virtue of the indication of this report. And that indicates that what is meant by this qadr is not the exposition. And as for interpreting qadr as knowledge, it is weak. Because if it is interpreted as any knowledge, then it is false. Evidence: that one who knows God's essence and attributes is not said to have 'qadr' of God's essence and attributes. And if it is interpreted as the knowledge that necessitates the occurrence of the act in a specific manner, then this necessitates that qadr only occurs with creation and bringing into being. And if it is interpreted as God's knowledge of the modality of the acts of servants, then compulsion (al-jabr) is also necessary. Because it is established that opposition to what God knows is impossible of occurrence. And as for his statement: 'Belief in qadr is carried upon that the creator of pleasures and pains is God', we say: this is weak. Because our statement: 'There is no god but God' is a declaration of denial of the two gods, and that indicates that the creator of benefits and harms is God. So when He obligated after that belief in qadr, it becomes necessary that what is meant by qadr is something else besides that. And that is nothing but belief that acts of obedience and evils are all from God. And as for his statement: 'What is meant by that is the affirmation that qadr is from the servant', we say: this is refuted by the fact that it has come in some narrations: 'And that you believe that qadr, its good and its evil, is from God.' And as for his statement: 'The doctrine of Ali ibn Abi Talib is the denial of compulsion (al-jabr), and if the narrator contradicts his narration it indicates weakness in the narration', we say: we do not concede that the doctrine of Ali is what you mentioned. And its verification will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of several interpretations of the term 'qadr' as it appears in a hadith. The author begins by establishing that belief in qadr is a condition of sound faith, then uses a logical argument (the second figure) to show that 'qadr' in that hadith does not mean the writing in the Preserved Tablet. Next, he rejects the interpretation of qadr as knowledge or speech (tongue), reasoning that if it were an exposition to humans or angels, it would not be an obligation of faith. He then attacks the interpretation of qadr as knowledge in general, offering a counterexample: knowing God's essence does not mean having 'qadr' of it. If it means specific knowledge that necessitates an act, then qadr only occurs with creation, which is problematic. If it means God's knowledge of the modality of acts, then compulsion (al-jabr) follows. The author then refutes the claim that belief in qadr is equivalent to believing that God creates pleasure and pain, noting that the declaration of tawhid already affirms that, so qadr must have another meaning: belief that all acts of obedience and evil come from God. He then rejects the view that qadr means affirmation of the servant's own power, citing narrations that state good and evil are from God. Finally, he dismisses the objection that Ali's doctrine denies compulsion, saying they do not accept that characterization and will address it later.

Key Terms
القدر (al-qadr)
اللوح المحفوظ (Preserved Tablet)
الإيمان (al-iman)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qadr refers to a pre-written book in heaven.
Correction: The author shows that the qadr required for faith is not that specific writing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is interpreting qadr as exposition to angels false?
A) Because angels already know everything.
B) Because believing that God explained servant actions to angels is not obligatory for faith.
C) Because the hadith does not mention angels.
D) Because exposition would imply compulsion.

Key Takeaway
The qadr that is required for faith is not the writing in the Preserved Tablet or any exposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something goes wrong in your life, do you truly submit to God's decree, or do you find yourself blaming luck, people, or your own mistakes? This lesson teaches that belief in qadr is a condition of sound faith—is your faith sound in that moment?
?
Have you ever secretly doubted that God's decree is just, especially when you faced hardship? The author argues that qadr is not about external records but about belief in God's decree—does your heart accept that decree without reservation?
?
If belief in qadr is obligatory, why do you still sometimes act as if your own planning controls outcomes? Where in your life today do you need to surrender to the reality that God's decree is what truly governs?

6 min
Qadr is Not Knowledge
The author refutes three ways of interpreting qadr as knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taking qadr as knowledge (in any sense) is weak and leads to problems.

Core Idea
Qadr cannot be reduced to knowledge without causing contradictions.

The author now tackles the interpretation that qadr means knowledge. He examines three possible meanings of 'knowledge' and shows each to be false or leading to undesirable consequences. This lesson helps you see the precision needed in defining qadr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the three interpretations of knowledge.
The author examines each interpretation separately.
2
Follow the three refutations
Identify each case: knowledge in general, knowledge that necessitates the act, and knowledge of modality.
Each leads to a problem.
3
Draw the conclusion
Understand why all three interpretations are rejected.
The last one leads to compulsion (jabr).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن الإيمان بالقدر من شرائط صحة الإيمان. وهذا ينتج من الشكل الثاني : أنه ليس المراد من القدر المذكور في هذا الخبر هو الكتابة في اللوح المحفوظ : وأما حمل القدر على العلم واللسان ، فباطل أيضاً . لأن ذلك البيان ، إما أن يكون بياناً للبشر ، وهو مفقود . أو للملائكة ، وهو أيضاً باطل . لأن العلم بأن الله بين أحوال أفعال العباد للملائكة ليس من واجبات الإيمان . وإيمان بالقدر من واجبات الإيمان بحكم دلالة هذا الخبر . وذلك يدل على أنه ليس المراد من هذا القدر هو البيان .
وأما حمل القدر على العلم ، فضعيف . لأنه إن حمل على أي علم كان ، فهو باطل . بدليل : أن من علم ذات الله وصفاته . لا يقال : إنه قدر ذات الله وصفاته ، وإن حمل على العلم المقتضي إيقاع الفعل على وجه خاص ، فهذا يقتضي أن لا يحصل القدر إلا مع التخليق والتكوين . وإن حمل على علم الله بكيفية أفعال العباد ، فالجبر أيضاً لازم. لما ثبت أن خلاف معلوم الله : محال

الله .

That belief in qadr is among the conditions of sound faith. And this results from the second figure: that what is meant by 'qadr' mentioned in this report is not the writing in the Preserved Tablet. And as for interpreting qadr as knowledge and the tongue, it is also false. Because that exposition is either an exposition to humans, which is absent, or to angels, which is also false. Because the knowledge that God explained the states of the actions of servants to the angels is not among the obligations of faith. And belief in qadr is among the obligations of faith by virtue of the indication of this report. And that indicates that what is meant by this qadr is not the exposition. And as for interpreting qadr as knowledge, it is weak. Because if it is interpreted as any knowledge, then it is false. And if it is interpreted as God's knowledge of the modality of the acts of servants, then compulsion (al-jabr) is also necessary. And as for his statement: 'Belief in qadr is carried upon that the creator of pleasures and pains is God', we say: this is weak. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of several interpretations of the term 'qadr' as it appears in a hadith. The author begins by establishing that belief in qadr is a condition of sound faith, then uses a logical argument (the second figure) to show that 'qadr' in that hadith does not mean the writing in the Preserved Tablet. Next, he rejects the interpretation of qadr as knowledge or speech (tongue), reasoning that if it were an exposition to humans or angels, it would not be an obligation of faith. He then attacks the interpretation of qadr as knowledge in general, offering a counterexample: knowing God's essence does not mean having 'qadr' of it. If it means specific knowledge that necessitates an act, then qadr only occurs with creation, which is problematic. If it means God's knowledge of the modality of acts, then compulsion (al-jabr) follows. The author then refutes the claim that belief in qadr is equivalent to believing that God creates pleasure and pain, noting that the declaration of tawhid already affirms that, so qadr must have another meaning: belief that all acts of obedience and evil come from God. He then rejects the view that qadr means affirmation of the servant's own power, citing narrations that state good and evil are from God. Finally, he dismisses the objection that Ali's doctrine denies compulsion, saying they do not accept that characterization and will address it later.

العلم (al-ilm)
التخليق والتكوين (creation and bringing into being)
الجبر (al-jabr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qadr equals God's knowledge in general.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to absurdity or compulsion.

Checkpoint
Which interpretation of qadr as knowledge does the author claim leads to compulsion?
A) Knowledge in general.
B) Knowledge that necessitates the act.
C) Knowledge of the modality of acts.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Qadr is not identical to God's knowledge; it involves more than mere knowing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If qadr is not just God's knowledge, but involves His active decree, how does that change the way you view your daily choices? Are you living as if God's decree is merely passive observation or as an active determination? This lesson calls you to examine whether you truly submit to God's will in your actions, not just in what He knows.
?
The author argues that interpreting qadr as knowledge leads to compulsion. If you believe God knows everything you will do, do you also feel compelled that you have no real choice? Where in your life do you use the excuse 'it was written' to avoid responsibility? Confront that tendency today.
?
Consider the three interpretations rejected in this passage. Which of these have you unconsciously adopted? Are you willing to let go of a view that makes God's decree merely intellectual and instead embrace a more active, sovereign decree? This lesson demands that you revere God's power, not just His knowledge.

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
Qadr Goes Beyond Tawhid
The author distinguishes qadr from general belief in God's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that qadr cannot be reduced to God creating pleasure and pain, because that is already covered by tawhid.

Core Idea
Belief in qadr adds something beyond affirming God as creator of all things.

Some have claimed that believing in qadr simply means acknowledging that God creates pleasure and pain. The author refutes this by pointing out that the declaration of tawhid already affirms that. Therefore, qadr must have a more specific meaning: belief that all acts of obedience and evil come from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the argument structure.
The author uses a comparison with the shahada.
2
Understand the redundancy argument
The author says that if qadr were just about God creating pleasure/pain, then the shahada already covers that, so qadr would be redundant.
3
Identify the specific meaning
The author concludes that qadr must mean something else: belief that acts of obedience and evil are from God.
This is the unique element of qadr.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن الإيمان بالقدر من شرائط صحة الإيمان. وهذا ينتج من الشكل الثاني : أنه ليس المراد من القدر المذكور في هذا الخبر هو الكتابة في اللوح المحفوظ : وأما حمل القدر على العلم واللسان ، فباطل أيضاً . لأن ذلك البيان ، إما أن يكون بياناً للبشر ، وهو مفقود . أو للملائكة ، وهو أيضاً باطل . لأن العلم بأن الله بين أحوال أفعال العباد للملائكة ليس من واجبات الإيمان . وإيمان بالقدر من واجبات الإيمان بحكم دلالة هذا الخبر . وذلك يدل على أنه ليس المراد من هذا القدر هو البيان .
وأما حمل القدر على العلم ، فضعيف . لأنه إن حمل على أي علم كان ، فهو باطل . بدليل : أن من علم ذات الله وصفاته . لا يقال : إنه قدر ذات الله وصفاته ، وإن حمل على العلم المقتضي إيقاع الفعل على وجه خاص ، فهذا يقتضي أن لا يحصل القدر إلا مع التخليق والتكوين . وإن حمل على علم الله بكيفية أفعال العباد ، فالجبر أيضاً لازم. لما ثبت أن خلاف معلوم الله : محال
الوقوع .
وأما قوله : « الإيمان بالقدر ، محمول على أن خالق اللذات والآلام ، هو الله ، قلنا : هذا ضعيف . لأن قولنا : لا إله إلا الله ، تصريح بنفي الإلمين وذلك يدل على أن خالق المنافع والمضار، هو الله . فلما أوجب بعده الإيمان بالقدر ، وجب أن يكون المراد من القدر شيئاً آخر ، سوى ذلك . وما ذاك إلا الإيمان بأن الطاعات والشرور كلها من الله .
وأما قوله : ( المراد من ذلك : إثبات أن القدر من العبد ، قلنا : هذا مدفوع بما أنه جاء في بعض الروايات : « وأن تؤمن بأن القدر خيره وشره من
الله .
وأما قوله : ( مذهب علي بن أبي طالب، نفي الجبر ، والراوي إذا خالف روايته دل على ضعف في الرواية ، قلنا : لا نسلم أن مذهب و علي » ما ذكرتم . وسيأتي تقريره ، إن شاء الله تعالى .
That belief in qadr is among the conditions of sound faith. And this results from the second figure: that what is meant by 'qadr' mentioned in this report is not the writing in the Preserved Tablet. And as for interpreting qadr as knowledge and the tongue, it is also false. Because that exposition is either an exposition to humans, which is absent, or to angels, which is also false. Because the knowledge that God explained the states of the actions of servants to the angels is not among the obligations of faith. And belief in qadr is among the obligations of faith by virtue of the indication of this report. And that indicates that what is meant by this qadr is not the exposition. And as for interpreting qadr as knowledge, it is weak. Because if it is interpreted as any knowledge, then it is false. Evidence: that one who knows God's essence and attributes is not said to have 'qadr' of God's essence and attributes. And if it is interpreted as the knowledge that necessitates the occurrence of the act in a specific manner, then this necessitates that qadr only occurs with creation and bringing into being. And if it is interpreted as God's knowledge of the modality of the acts of servants, then compulsion (al-jabr) is also necessary. Because it is established that opposition to what God knows is impossible of occurrence. And as for his statement: 'Belief in qadr is carried upon that the creator of pleasures and pains is God', we say: this is weak. Because our statement: 'There is no god but God' is a declaration of denial of the two gods, and that indicates that the creator of benefits and harms is God. So when He obligated after that belief in qadr, it becomes necessary that what is meant by qadr is something else besides that. And that is nothing but belief that acts of obedience and evils are all from God. And as for his statement: 'What is meant by that is the affirmation that qadr is from the servant', we say: this is refuted by the fact that it has come in some narrations: 'And that you believe that qadr, its good and its evil, is from God.' And as for his statement: 'The doctrine of Ali ibn Abi Talib is the denial of compulsion (al-jabr), and if the narrator contradicts his narration it indicates weakness in the narration', we say: we do not concede that the doctrine of Ali is what you mentioned. And its verification will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of several interpretations of the term 'qadr' as it appears in a hadith. The author begins by establishing that belief in qadr is a condition of sound faith, then uses a logical argument (the second figure) to show that 'qadr' in that hadith does not mean the writing in the Preserved Tablet. Next, he rejects the interpretation of qadr as knowledge or speech (tongue), reasoning that if it were an exposition to humans or angels, it would not be an obligation of faith. He then attacks the interpretation of qadr as knowledge in general, offering a counterexample: knowing God's essence does not mean having 'qadr' of it. If it means specific knowledge that necessitates an act, then qadr only occurs with creation, which is problematic. If it means God's knowledge of the modality of acts, then compulsion (al-jabr) follows. The author then refutes the claim that belief in qadr is equivalent to believing that God creates pleasure and pain, noting that the declaration of tawhid already affirms that, so qadr must have another meaning: belief that all acts of obedience and evil come from God. He then rejects the view that qadr means affirmation of the servant's own power, citing narrations that state good and evil are from God. Finally, he dismisses the objection that Ali's doctrine denies compulsion, saying they do not accept that characterization and will address it later.

Key Terms
اللذات والآلام (pleasures and pains)
المنافع والمضار (benefits and harms)
الطاعات والشرور (acts of obedience and evils)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qadr is redundant with the shahada.
Correction: The author shows that qadr adds a specific nuance about the origin of actions.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the specific meaning of qadr beyond the shahada?
A) That God creates pleasures and pains.
B) That acts of obedience and evils are from God.
C) That God knows everything.
D) That the servant has power.

Key Takeaway
Qadr specifically includes belief that both acts of obedience and evil are from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your sins are from God's decree, or do you secretly resent God when you commit them? How does this belief change how you respond to your own disobedience?
?
If you truly believed that both obedience and evil come from God, would you still feel pride in your good deeds? Would you despair at your sins? How should this affect your du'a?
?
The author argues shahada is not enough; qadr adds something specific. Have you ever stopped at affirming Allah as Creator of everything, without accepting that your own choices are under His decree? What difference would that make in your life today?

Qadr is From God, Not the Servant
The author refutes the interpretation that qadr is from the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the claim that qadr means affirming the servant's own power.

Core Idea
Qadr is from God, not from the servant's own power.

Some argued that qadr means affirming that the servant has power (qadr). The author counters by citing hadith narrations that explicitly state that qadr, both its good and evil, is from God. This lesson emphasizes the divine origin of all qadr.

Read the passage

2
Identify the refutation
The author cites a narration that says qadr (good and evil) is from God.
This directly contradicts the opponent.
3
Draw the conclusion
Understand that qadr's origin is divine, not human.

Ali’s Doctrine on Qadr is Not as Claimed
The author denies the claimed doctrine of Ali and postpones proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response to the appeal to Ali's supposed denial of compulsion.

Core Idea
Ali's doctrine is not what the opponent claims; it will be explained later.

The opponent invokes Ali's authority to reject compulsion. The author responds by denying that this is Ali's true position and promises a later verification. This lesson shows how the author handles appeals to authority.

Guided Path

Read the passage

2
Understand the author's response

This is a deferral, not a detailed rebuttal.

Third Proof: Hadith on Creation and Two Arguments for Predestination
The author cites the hadith and derives two arguments for predestination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for predestination based on the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud and the two rational arguments derived from it, which together refute the Mu'tazilite doctrine of free will.

Core Idea
God's decree is irrevocable; no deed can override what has been written.

This lesson presents a key hadith on predestination and two rational arguments that follow from it. The hadith describes the angelic writing of fate and two dramatic cases of last-minute changes. The author uses these to prove that human will cannot override God's decree, refuting the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic hadith and its translation. Notice the detailed description of fetal development and the four things written by the angel.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim.
2
Identify the two case studies
The hadith gives two examples: someone near Paradise overtaken by the writing, and someone near Hell preceded by the writing. Note the pattern.
3
Understand the first argument
The first argument uses logical impossibility: if God decreed someone wretched, he cannot become blessed, because that would make God's report false and His knowledge ignorance.
This argument relies on the principle that God's report is truthful and His knowledge is perfect.
4
Understand the second argument
The second argument shows that the servant's deeds can change due to the prior writing, meaning he has no power to oppose God's decree.
5
Connect to Mu'tazilite refutation
Reflect on how both arguments together refute the Mu'tazilite belief in free will, which asserts that humans create their own actions independently.
The Mu'tazila would say that destiny can change based on deeds, but the hadith shows the opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : روى الشيخان في الصحيحين بإسنادهما عن الأعمش ، عن زيد بن وهب ، قال : سمعت عبد الله بن مسعود ، يقول : حدثنا رسول الله عليه السلام ، وهو الصادق المصدوق : ( إن خلق أحدكم يجمع في بطن أمه ، أربعين يوماً نطفة ، ثم يكون علقة مثل ذلك ، ثم يكون مضغة مثل ذلك ، ثم يبعث الله إليه الملك بأربع كلمات . فيكتب رزقه وعمله وأجله وشقي أو سعيد ، قال : ( وإن أحدكم ليعمل بعمل أهل الجنة ، حتى ما يكون بينها وبينه إلا ذراع، فيبق عليه الكتاب ، فيعمل بعمل أهل النار ، فيدخلها . وإن أحدكم ليعمل بعمل أهل النار ، حتى ما يكون بينه وبينها إلا ذراع، فيسبق عليه الكتاب ، فيعمل بعمل أهل الجنة ، فيدخلها ) .
والاستدلال بهذا الخبر من وجهين :
الأول : إنه تعالى لما حكم به بأنه شقي ، امتنع أن يصير سعيداً ، وإلا لزم أن ينقلب الخبر الصدق : كذباً ، وأن ينقلب العلم : جهلا . وكل ذلك
محال
الثاني : إنه عليه السلام بين أن الرجل يعمل بعمل أهل النار ، إلا أنه لأجل أنه سبق الكتاب بكونه من أهل الجنة ، ينقلب من عمل أهل النار إلى عمل أهل الجنة . وهذا يدل على أنه لا قدرة للعبد ، على خلاف حكم الله
وعلمه . وذلك يبطل القول بالاعتزال .
The third proof: The two shaykhs narrated in the two Sahihs with their chain from al-A'mash, from Zayd ibn Wahb, who said: I heard Abdullah ibn Mas'ud say: The Messenger of God, upon him peace, the truthful and believed, narrated to us: 'The creation of each one of you is gathered in his mother's womb for forty days as a drop, then becomes a clot for like that, then becomes a lump of flesh for like that, then God sends the angel with four words: he writes his provision, his deeds, his term, and whether he is wretched or blessed.' He said: 'And indeed, one of you performs the deeds of the people of Paradise until there is between him and it only a cubit, then the writing overtakes him, and he performs the deeds of the people of the Fire and enters it. And indeed, one of you performs the deeds of the people of the Fire until there is between him and it only a cubit, then the writing precedes him, and he performs the deeds of the people of Paradise and enters it.' And the inference from this report is in two ways: The first: Since God has judged that he is wretched, it is impossible that he becomes blessed, otherwise it would necessitate that the truthful report turns into a lie, and that knowledge turns into ignorance. All of that is impossible. The second: He, peace be upon him, explained that a man performs the deeds of the people of the Fire, but because the writing preceded that he is from the people of Paradise, he turns from the deeds of the people of the Fire to the deeds of the people of Paradise. And this indicates that the servant has no power contrary to God's judgment and knowledge. And that invalidates the doctrine of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for predestination, consisting of a hadith from Ibn Mas'ud and two arguments derived from it. The hadith describes the development of the fetus and the angel writing the person's provision, deeds, lifespan, and final state of wretchedness or blessedness. It also narrates the case of someone near Paradise but overtaken by the writing, and someone near Hell but preceded by the writing. The first argument: Since God's judgment (hukm) is that a person is wretched, it is impossible for him to become blessed, because that would turn God's true report into a lie and His knowledge into ignorance—both impossible. The second argument: The hadith explicitly shows that a person can be doing deeds of Hell until just before death, but if the writing (i.e., God's decree) has preceded that he is from Paradise, he changes to deeds of Paradise. This demonstrates that the servant has no power to oppose God's decree and knowledge, thus refuting the Mu'tazilite doctrine of free will.

Key Terms
القدر (al-qadr)
الكتاب (al-kitab)
السابق (al-sabiq)
الخبر الصدق (the truthful report)
العلم (al-ilm)
الاعتزال (al-i'tizal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith allows for a change in destiny based on deeds, implying free will.
Correction: The author uses the hadith to show that the final outcome is predetermined and unchangeable; the examples illustrate that the prior writing overrides any deeds, refuting free will.

Checkpoint
What is the second inference (al-thani) drawn from the hadith?
A) That God's knowledge cannot change.
B) That a person's deeds can change his destiny.
C) That the servant has no power contrary to God's decree and knowledge.
D) That the writing is only for the blessed.

Key Takeaway
The hadith and its two arguments firmly establish that God's decree is unchangeable and that human will is subordinate to it, thereby rejecting Mu'tazilite free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The hadith says the final state is written before birth. Does that reality fill you with fear or hope? How does it affect the way you approach your last moments—do you beg for a good end more than you strive for good deeds?
?
If you truly have no power contrary to God's judgment and knowledge, then how should you respond when you sin or fail? Do you despair, or does it drive you to rely solely on God's mercy? What needs to change in your heart right now?

The Hadith on Fetal Creation and Predestination
Narrates the hadith on fetal development and its two reversal stories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the prophetic hadith on the stages of fetal development and how it establishes the foundation for divine foreordination.

Core Idea
The hadith shows that God's decree precedes and overrides human action at the final moment.

This lesson presents a well-known prophetic tradition describing the formation of a human being in the womb and the recording of his destiny. The hadith is central to the Ash'ari argument for predestination because it illustrates how a person's final outcome can be reversed by God's prior decree, even when his actions seem to indicate otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage of the hadith once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the detailed stages of creation and the angel's writing.
The hadith describes four stages: nutfah, clot, lump, and then the sending of the angel.
2
Identify the key events
List the stages of fetal development and the four things the angel writes. Also note the two reversal examples given by the Prophet.
The reversal examples show how the decree can override a person's apparent actions just before death.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Think about why this hadith is used to support predestination. What does it say about human ability to change destiny?
The hadith implies that God's prior decree is decisive, but does not necessarily negate human responsibility in Ash'ari thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن زيد بن وهب ، قال : سمعت عبد الله بن مسعود ، يقول : حدثنا رسول الله عليه السلام ، وهو الصادق المصدوق : ( إن خلق أحدكم يجمع في بطن أمه ، أربعين يوماً نطفة ، ثم يكون علقة مثل ذلك ، ثم يكون مضغة مثل ذلك ، ثم يبعث الله إليه الملك بأربع كلمات . فيكتب رزقه وعمله وأجله وشقي أو سعيد ، قال : ( وإن أحدكم ليعمل بعمل أهل الجنة ، حتى ما يكون بينها وبينه إلا ذراع، فيبق عليه الكتاب ، فيعمل بعمل أهل النار ، فيدخلها . وإن أحدكم ليعمل بعمل أهل النار ، حتى ما يكون بينه وبينها إلا ذراع، فيسبق عليه الكتاب ، فيعمل بعمل أهل الجنة ، فيدخلها ) . والاستدلال بهذا الخبر من وجهين : الأول : إنه تعالى لما حكم به بأنه شقي ، امتنع أن يصير سعيداً ، وإلا لزم أن ينقلب الخبر الصدق : كذباً ، وأن ينقلب العلم : جهلا . وكل ذلك محال . الثاني : إنه عليه السلام بين أن الرجل يعمل بعمل أهل النار ، إلا أنه لأجل أنه سبق الكتاب بكونه من أهل الجنة ، ينقلب من عمل أهل النار إلى عمل أهل الجنة . وهذا يدل على أنه لا قدرة للعبد ، على خلاف حكم الله وعلمه . وذلك يبطل القول بالاعتزال .
From Zaid bin Wahb, he said: I heard Abdullah bin Mas'ud say: The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, told us—and he is the truthful and believed one: (The creation of each one of you is gathered in his mother's womb for forty days as a drop, then becomes a clot for a similar period, then becomes a lump for a similar period, then God sends to him the angel with four words, and he writes his provision, his deeds, his lifespan, and whether he is wretched or blessed. He said: And verily one of you does the deeds of the people of Paradise until there is only a cubit between him and it, but the decree overtakes him, and he does the deeds of the people of Hell and enters it. And verily one of you does the deeds of the people of Hell until there is only a cubit between him and it, but the decree precedes him, and he does the deeds of the people of Paradise and enters it.) And the inference from this report is in two aspects: First: Since He, Exalted, judged him to be wretched, it is impossible for him to become blessed, otherwise the truthful report would have to turn into a lie, and knowledge would turn into ignorance, and all that is impossible. Second: He, peace be upon him, explained that a man does the deeds of the people of Hell, but because the decree preceded that he is among the people of Paradise, he turns from the deeds of the people of Hell to the deeds of the people of Paradise. This indicates that the servant has no power to oppose God's decree and knowledge, and that invalidates the Mu'tazilite doctrine.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents a hadith and two proofs derived from it to support the Ash'ari position on predestination (jabr). The hadith describes the stages of fetal development and the recording of destiny, then narrates two examples of someone whose final outcome is reversed by the divine decree just before death. The first proof argues that since God's judgment of wretchedness is absolute, it cannot change, otherwise divine knowledge would become ignorance—an impossibility. The second proof points out that a person may be doing deeds of Hell, but because God's decree has already written him as blessed, he changes to deeds of Paradise, showing that human action is ultimately subject to God's will, not independent. The author concludes that this refutes Mu'tazilism, which asserts human free will and ability to oppose God's decree.

Correction: The hadith illustrates God's foreknowledge and decree, but Ash'ari theology maintains human acquisition (kasb) and moral responsibility alongside divine predestination.

Two Logical Proofs from the Hadith for Predestination
Derives two logical proofs from the hadith to support predestination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments derived from the hadith to prove that the servant has no power to oppose God's decree.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge could change, it would become ignorance — that is impossible.

The hadith is not merely a story; it contains two logical proofs that support the Ash'ari position against Mu'tazilite free will. The first proof argues from the impossibility of God's knowledge changing, and the second from the observed reversal of actions due to the prior decree. This lesson will break down each proof and show how they refute the Mu'tazilite doctrine of human autonomy.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first proof
Read the first inference: if God decreed someone as wretched, he cannot become blessed, because that would make God's report false and His knowledge ignorance. Understand the logic.
This proof relies on the impossibility of change in God's attributes.
2
Examine the second proof
Read the second inference: a person may be doing deeds of Hell but due to prior decree, he changes to deeds of Paradise. This shows the servant has no power to oppose God's decree.
This proof uses the example from the hadith to argue for the lack of independent human power.
3
Connect to refutation of Mu'tazilism
See how these proofs invalidate the Mu'tazilite belief that humans create their own actions independently of God's decree.
Mu'tazilites hold that humans have free will and can oppose God's will, but these proofs show that cannot be the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And verily one of you does the deeds of the people of Hell until there is only a cubit between him and it, but the decree precedes him, and he does the deeds of the people of Paradise and enters it.) Second: He, peace be upon him, explained that a man does the deeds of the people of Hell, but because the decree preceded that he is among the people of Paradise, he turns from the deeds of the people of Hell to the deeds of the people of Paradise. Three Objections to the Hadith on Predestination
Presents three objections: theological, empirical, and logical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three distinct objections—theological, empirical, and logical—raised against the hadith on predestination in al-Matalib al-Aliyah.

Core Idea
Three objections that attack the hadith from different angles: authority, science, and logic.

The hadith about predestination appears problematic to some. This passage records three forceful objections: the first from a Mu'tazili scholar rejecting the chain entirely, the second from medical observation contradicting the stages of embryonic development, and the third from logical absurdity if predestination excuses disbelief. Each objection aims to show the hadith cannot be authentic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
Read the passage and list the three separate objections. Notice how each begins with a distinct argument: authority, observation, logic.
2
Examine the first objection
The first is a quote from 'Amr bin 'Ubaid. Notice his escalating rejection: from the chain of narrators up to God Himself.
He uses a rhetorical crescendo to show the hadith is unacceptable at every level.
3
Examine the second objection
This objection uses empirical evidence from medical observations and miscarriages to argue the timeline is wrong.
It claims a complete fetus forms in 40 days, contradicting the hadith's 120-day process.
4
Examine the third objection
This is a logical reduction: if predestination is absolute, then all disbelievers are excused, which undermines the purpose of prophethood itself. Therefore the premise is false.
This is an example of reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا الخبر مشتمل على أمور باطلة ، فوجب أن يقال : إنه ليس كلاماً لرسول الله . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : حكى الخطيب في ( تاريخ بغداد ، عن : عمرو بن عبيد ) قال : «لو سمعت الأعمش يقول هذا ، لكذبته ، ولو سمعت زيد بن وهب يقول هذا ، ما أجبته ، ولو سمعت عبد الله بن مسعود يقول هذا ، ما قبلته ، ولو سمعت رسول الله يقوله لرددته ، ولو سمعت الله يقول هذا ، لقلت : ليس على هذا أخذت ميثاقاً . ( الثاني : وهو أن هذا الحديث يدل على أن النطقة تبقى نطفة أربعين يوماً ، ثم تبقى علقة أربعين يوماً ، ثم تبقى مضغة أربعين يوماً . والمشاهدات الطبية دلت على أن الأمر ليس كذلك . فإنه في مدة أربعين يوماً يتكون الولد بتمامه ، ومشاهدة السقط تدل على هذه الأحوال . وإذا كان هذا الخبر مشتملاً على الخطأ ، وجب تنزيه منصب النبوة عنه . الثالث : إن الأمر لو كان كما قرره في كتاب ( الجبر ) وجب أن يكون الكفار كلهم معذورين . وفي ذلك بطلان نبوته ورسالته ، والفرع إذا أدى إلى فساد الأصل ، كان باطلاً .
For it is said: This report contains false things, so it must be said that it is not the speech of the Messenger of God. Explanation from aspects: First: Al-Khatib narrated in 'Tarikh Baghdad' from 'Amr bin 'Ubaid that he said: 'If I heard Al-A'mash say this, I would call him a liar; if I heard Zaid bin Wahb say this, I would not answer him; if I heard Abdullah bin Mas'ud say this, I would not accept it; if I heard the Messenger of God say it, I would reject it; if I heard God say this, I would say: Not on this did I take a covenant.' Second: This hadith indicates that the drop remains a drop for forty days, then remains a clot for forty days, then remains a lump for forty days. But medical observations show that it is not so, for in a period of forty days the child is completely formed, and observing miscarriages indicates these states. Since this report contains error, it is necessary to exonerate the station of prophethood from it. Third: If the matter were as he established in the book (Al-Jabr), then all disbelievers would be excused. In that is the invalidation of his prophethood and message, and if a branch leads to corruption of the root, it is false.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the hadith. The first objection is a historical quote from the Mu'tazili 'Amr bin 'Ubaid, who rejects the hadith chain by chain, even hypothetically rejecting it if from God, because he considers its content to contradict the divine covenant. The second objection is empirical: medical observations show that embryonic development does not take 120 days in stages as the hadith implies; rather, a complete fetus forms within 40 days. Thus the hadith contains factual error and cannot be from a prophet. The third objection is logical: if absolute predestination (jabr) were true, then all disbelievers would be excused for their disbelief, which would negate the purpose of prophethood and the message itself—a self-defeating conclusion. The objector argues that a claim (predestination) that leads to the invalidation of the very prophethood that asserted it must be false.

Key Terms
أمور باطلة
الأعمش
عمرو بن عبيد
المشاهدات الطبية
السقط
تنزيه منصب النبوة
الفرع إذا أدى إلى فساد الأصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objections simply reject the hadith out of hand without method.
Correction: Each objection uses a distinct method: theological rejection, empirical falsification, and logical reduction ad absurdum.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the second objection against the hadith?
A) The hadith contradicts a consensus of scholars
B) The hadith describes the drop, clot, and lump stages but medical evidence suggests the fetus is fully formed within 40 days
C) The hadith claims the angel writes only for Muslims
D) The hadith denies human free will

Key Takeaway
The three objections demonstrate that the hadith is attacked from multiple angles: its chain, its factual accuracy, and its logical implications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a hadith or verse that seems to conflict with your understanding or empirical evidence, do you react like 'Amr bin 'Ubaid—rejecting it outright—or do you seek to reconcile? Which approach is more faithful to our deen, and what does your usual reaction reveal about your heart's submission to revelation?
?
The third objection argues that absolute predestination would excuse disbelief. Honestly examine yourself: when you fall short in your obligations, do you ever quietly blame qadar or God's decree? When was the last time you used fate as a shield against personal accountability, and what would it take for you to own your choices fully?
?
The objectors claim to exonerate prophethood by rejecting the hadith. Are you willing to accept that some things in revelation may seem problematic to human reason? Where is the line between defending the truth and being defensive—and how do you know which side you are on when your comfort is challenged?

Divine Decree

Defense of the Hadith and Conditional Decree
Accepts the hadith, reinterprets it, and refutes the objection using conditional decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the hadith about predestination while preserving human free will through the concept of conditional decree.

Core Idea
God's decree is conditional, not absolute, preserving both knowledge and human choice.

The author engages with a hadith that seems to imply absolute predestination. Instead of rejecting it, he accepts its authenticity but reinterprets its meaning. He argues that the hadith only affirms God's eternal knowledge, not that humans lack power. Then he introduces a crucial distinction: God's decrees are conditional, so human choices remain real. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the full meaning. Notice the structure: acceptance, reinterpretation, refutation, and introduction of conditional decree.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the author's three moves
Identify three parts: 1) Acceptance of the hadith (سلمنا), 2) Reinterpretation that it only indicates God's knowledge, not human inability, 3) Introduction of conditional decree (الحكم المشروط).
3
Understand the refutation of the objection
Focus on the objection: if the known event does not occur, God's knowledge would become ignorance. The author argues this is a general problem, not specific to this hadith. Also, using the hadith to prove God knows particulars leads to circularity.
The author uses logical reasoning to show the objection is not unique.
4
Reflect on conditional decree
Consider how conditional decrees solve the problem: God's decree is conditional (if...then), so if the servant's action changes, the condition changes, and the decree's result changes accordingly. This does not imply change in God's knowledge.
This preserves both divine foreknowledge and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : صحة الحديث. لكن الحديث لا يدل إلا على أن جميع العباد بعلم الله وبحكمه. وذلك لا نزاع فيه . لأن مذهبنا أنه تعالى كان عالماً في الأزل ، بأن « زيداً ، سيكون مؤمناً مطيعاً ، وأن ( عمرو ، سيكون فاسقاً كافراً . وأنه لا يصدر عنها إلا ما علم الله صدوره منها، وهذا القدر لا يدل على أنه لا قدرة للعبد على خلافه، لأن العلم لا يقلب الممكن عمالاً ، ولا يخرجه عن حد الجواز . أما قوله : لو لم يحصل ذلك المعلوم لا نقلب العلم جهلا ، والصدق كذباً ، قلنا : لما رجع حاصل الاستدلال إلى هذا الحرف ، لم يبق لخصوصية هذا الخبر فائدة فإنه يقال : إنه تعالى علم من كل أحد فعله المعين ، فوجب أن لا يقدر على تركه ، وإلا لزم انقلاب علمه جهلا . ولا يقال : إنا نستدل بهذا الخبر على أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات - لأنا نقول : إثبات النبوة فرع لهذه المسألة فلو أثبتناها ، نقول : النبي لزمه الدور . وإنه محال . ثم نقول : الإشكال الذي ذكرتم إنما يلزم لو حكم الله بأفعال العباد حكماً مطلقاً . وهذا ممنوع . بل عندنا : أنه تعالى يحكم بها مشروطاً ، فيقول : إن صدرت الطاعة عن ( زيد ) دخل الجنة ، وإن صدرت المعصية عنه دخل النار . وإذا كان هذا الحكم مشروطاً ، لم يلزم من تغير فعل العبد : تغير علم الله ، وتغير حكمه .
We accept the authenticity of the hadith. But the hadith only indicates that all servants are under God's knowledge and decree, and there is no dispute about that. Because our doctrine is that He, Exalted, knew from eternity that Zaid would be a believing obedient person, and that 'Amr would be a disobedient disbeliever, and that nothing proceeds from them except what God knew would proceed from them. This amount does not indicate that the servant has no power to oppose it, because knowledge does not turn the possible into an actual (impossible), nor does it remove it from the realm of permissibility. As for his saying: 'If that known thing did not occur, then knowledge would turn into ignorance and truth into falsehood,' we say: Since the gist of the inference reduces to this point, there remains no benefit specific to this report, for it is said: God knows from everyone his specific action, so he must be unable to abandon it, otherwise His knowledge would turn into ignorance. And it is not said: We infer from this report that God knows particulars—because we say: Establishing prophethood is subsidiary to this issue; if we establish it [by appealing to the prophet], then the prophet would be subject to circularity, and that is impossible. Then we say: The difficulty you mentioned would only follow if God decrees the actions of servants absolutely. But that is denied. Rather, in our view, He decrees them conditionally, saying: If obedience issues from Zaid, he enters Paradise; if disobedience issues from him, he enters Hell. And when the decree is conditional, it does not follow that a change in the servant's action necessitates a change in God's knowledge and decree.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is the response to the objections. The author first accepts the hadith's authenticity (sallamna) and then reinterprets its theological implications. The key point is that the hadith only affirms God's eternal knowledge of what will happen, not that humans lack capacity. Knowledge does not make the possible impossible; it simply knows which possibility will occur. The author then refutes the objection that if the known event does not occur, God's knowledge would become ignorance: he argues that this is a general philosophical problem, not specific to this hadith. Moreover, one cannot use the hadith to prove that God knows particulars because that would be circular (the prophet established by the hadith would then be used to prove the very issue). Finally, the author introduces the concept of conditional decree: God's judgment (e.g., 'if he obeys, he enters Paradise') is conditional, so if the action changes, the condition changes, and the decree changes accordingly—this preserves both divine knowledge and human responsibility.

Key Terms
سلمنا
علم الله في الأزل
العلم لا يقلب الممكن
الاستدلال بالخبر
الدور
الحكم المشروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith proves absolute predestination and negates human free will.
Correction: The hadith only affirms God's eternal knowledge, not that humans are compelled. God's decrees are conditional, so human choices remain real.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that the hadith proves absolute predestination?
A) By denying the hadith's authenticity
B) By arguing that God's knowledge does not make the possible impossible, and that God's decree is conditional
C) By claiming the hadith applies only to the afterlife
D) By citing another hadith

Key Takeaway
The hadith does not negate human free will; God's decree is conditional, preserving both divine knowledge and human capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever silently excuse yourself by thinking, 'God already knew I would do this, so I couldn't have avoided it'? How does the author's claim that 'knowledge does not turn the possible into impossible' challenge that excuse? Will you still use that excuse after today?
?
If you truly believed that God's decree for your life is conditional—that your choices determine the outcomes—would you pray differently? Would you struggle harder against a recurring sin? What is holding you back from living with that conviction right now?
?
The author warns against circular reasoning: using prophetic hadith to prove something that itself is needed to believe the prophet. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on a similar circular comfort—believing something because of a source you haven't fully accepted? Will you examine those assumptions today?

Fatalism Excuses Disbelievers? The Third Argument and Its Response
Objection against fatalism and layered response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that fatalism excuses all disbelievers is refuted by distinguishing divine foreknowledge from compulsion and by emphasizing conditional divine judgments.

Core Idea
Foreknowledge is not force; conditional decrees preserve accountability.

This lesson presents a crucial objection to fatalism: if God predestines all actions, then disbelievers have an excuse, which would undermine the very purpose of prophethood. The response defends orthodox Sunni theology by clarifying that divine knowledge does not compel human action and that God's decrees are conditional on the servant's choice. Understanding this argument helps us see why fatalism is rationally and theologically untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice how the objector states the problem and how the response unfolds step by step.
The passage uses concrete examples (Zayd, Amr) to illustrate abstract points.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objector's argument: if all actions are compelled (jabr), then disbelievers are excused, which would invalidate prophethood. Note the 'branch corrupts the root' logic.
3
Understand the initial concession
The response concedes the hadith but reinterprets it: it only says God knows actions, not that He compels them. Note how knowledge and compulsion are separated.
Key phrase: 'العلم لا يقلب الممكن عمالاً' – knowledge does not turn the possible into actual.
4
Follow the reduction and circularity argument
The objector's point is reduced to a general claim about all divine knowledge. Then the response points out a circularity: establishing prophethood depends on this issue, so using it to argue would be circular.
This shows the logical depth of the response.
5
Grasp the conditional decree solution
The final and key point: God's judgments are conditional ('مشروطاً'), not absolute. If the servant's action changes, the judgment changes accordingly, so no change in God's knowledge or decree is implied.
This resolves the problem without fatalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الأمر لو كان كما قرره في كتاب ( الجبر ) وجب أن يكون الكفار كلهم معذورين . وفي ذلك بطلان نبوته ورسالته ، والفرع إذا أدى إلى فساد الأصل ، كان باطلاً . سلمنا : صحة الحديث. لكن الحديث لا يدل إلا على أن جميع العباد بعلم الله وبحكمه. وذلك لا نزاع فيه . لأن مذهبنا أنه تعالى كان عالماً في الأزل ، بأن « زيداً ، سيكون مؤمناً مطيعاً ، وأن ( عمرو ، سيكون فاسقاً كافراً . وأنه لا يصدر عنها إلا ما علم الله صدوره منها، وهذا القدر لا يدل على أنه لا قدرة للعبد على خلافه، لأن العلم لا يقلب الممكن عمالاً ، ولا يخرجه عن حد الجواز . أما قوله : لو لم يحصل ذلك المعلوم لا نقلب العلم جهلا ، والصدق كذباً ، قلنا : لما رجع حاصل الاستدلال إلى هذا الحرف ، لم يبق لخصوصية هذا الخبر فائدة فإنه يقال : إنه تعالى علم من كل أحد فعله المعين ، فوجب أن لا يقدر على تركه ، وإلا لزم انقلاب علمه جهلا . ولا يقال : إنا نستدل بهذا الخبر على أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات - لأنا نقول : إثبات النبوة فرع لهذه المسألة فلو أثبتناها ، نقول : النبي لزمه الدور . وإنه محال . ثم نقول : الإشكال الذي ذكرتم إنما يلزم لو حكم الله بأفعال العباد حكماً مطلقاً . وهذا ممنوع . بل عندنا : أنه تعالى يحكم بها مشروطاً ، فيقول : إن صدرت الطاعة عن ( زيد ) دخل الجنة ، وإن صدرت المعصية عنه دخل النار . وإذا كان هذا الحكم مشروطاً ، لم يلزم من تغير فعل العبد : تغير علم الله ، وتغير حكمه
Third: If the matter were as he established in the book (Al-Jabr), then all disbelievers would necessarily be excused. In that is the nullification of his prophethood and his message, and if a branch leads to the corruption of the root, it is invalid. We concede: the authenticity of the hadith. But the hadith only indicates that all servants are by God's knowledge and His decree. There is no dispute about that. For our doctrine is that He, exalted, was knowing in eternity, that 'Zayd will be a believing obedient person, and Amr will be a corrupt disbeliever. And that nothing issues from them except what God knew would issue from them, and this extent does not indicate that the servant has no power to do otherwise, because knowledge does not turn the possible into an actual, nor does it take it out of the realm of permissibility. As for his statement: If that known thing did not occur, knowledge would turn into ignorance and truth into falsehood. We say: Since the conclusion of the argument reduces to this word, there remains no benefit specific to this report, for it is said: He, exalted, knew from everyone his specific action, so he must not be able to leave it, otherwise his knowledge would turn into ignorance. And it cannot be said: We infer from this report that He, exalted, knows particulars—because we say: Establishing prophethood is subsidiary to this issue, so if we establish it, we say: the prophet would suffer from circularity, and it is impossible. Then we say: The difficulty you mentioned would only follow if God judged the actions of servants absolutely. This is denied. Rather, in our view: He, exalted, judges them conditionally, saying: If obedience issues from Zayd, he enters paradise, and if disobedience issues from him, he enters hellfire. And if this judgment is conditional, then a change in the servant's action does not necessitate a change in God's knowledge or His judgment.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against fatalism (الجبر) and the response to it. The objector argues that if fatalism were true (as supposedly held by the author of the book Al-Jabr), then all disbelievers would be excused, which would invalidate prophethood—since a branch (the consequence) that leads to the corruption of the root (prophethood itself) is invalid. The response concedes the hadith's authenticity but reinterprets it: the hadith only indicates that God knows all actions, not that He compels them. The author's school holds that God eternally knows that Zayd will be a believer and Amr a disbeliever, but this knowledge does not strip the servant of power to do otherwise, because knowledge does not change the nature of the possible; it merely knows what will happen. The response addresses the objection that if the known thing did not occur, God's knowledge would become ignorance; it says this is a general argument that applies to all divine knowledge, not specifically to this hadith, and it does not entail fatalism. Furthermore, establishing prophethood depends on the issue of divine knowledge, so using that argument would lead to circularity. Finally, the response clarifies that the difficulty only arises if God's decree is absolute and unconditional. But in the author's view, God's judgments are conditional: if Zayd obeys, he enters paradise; if he disobeys, hellfire. Thus, a change in the servant's action does not imply a change in God's knowledge or decree, because the decree was conditional from the beginning.

Key Terms
الجبر
العلم الأزلي
الحكم المشروط
الممكن
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine foreknowledge eliminates human free will and makes God's decree absolute and unchanging.
Correction: Foreknowledge is mere awareness, not compulsion; God's decrees are conditional on human actions, so accountability remains.

Checkpoint
How does the response avoid the implication that disbelievers are excused under fatalism?
A) By rejecting the authenticity of the hadith.
B) By asserting that God's judgment is conditional on the servant's action.
C) By claiming that God does not know future actions.
D) By arguing that disbelievers have no choice.

Key Takeaway
The fatalist objection fails because divine foreknowledge does not compel action, and God's conditional judgments preserve human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever quietly excused a sin by telling yourself that God already knew you would do it? How does this lesson's reminder that 'knowledge does not turn the possible into an actual' strip that excuse of its power? Commit to catching yourself in that thought and repeating this lesson's truth.
?
The passage says God's decrees are conditional: if you obey, paradise; if you disobey, hellfire. Does that conditional nature make you feel more responsible or more anxious? Be honest—then ask yourself: if I truly believed my choices matter eternally, what one habit would I change starting today?
?
When you fall short in worship, do you ever sense a whisper that it was 'written' and you can't change? This lesson argues that foreknowledge is not force. What specific area of your life have you surrendered to that fatalist whisper? Name it now, and decide one concrete action you will take this week to exercise your God-given power to choose obedience.
